<div id="review_370398533" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/370398533" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4015835-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420385695p2/4015835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/370398533?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4015835-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=arc">arc</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer370398533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer289441460923226808">I recently read The Passage this year so I was lucky I didn’t have to wait as long as everyone else for book two; The Twelve. I was privileged to win an advanced review copy so I need to be careful in reviewing this book without any spoilers. As many people would already know The Twelve continues on with the epic tale that The Passage built. This time we have a whole lot of new characters to read about as they struggle to survive in this nightmarish world of virals.<br/><br/>One of my biggest issues with </span>
  <span id="freeText289441460923226808" style="display:none">I recently read The Passage this year so I was lucky I didn’t have to wait as long as everyone else for book two; The Twelve. I was privileged to win an advanced review copy so I need to be careful in reviewing this book without any spoilers. As many people would already know The Twelve continues on with the epic tale that The Passage built. This time we have a whole lot of new characters to read about as they struggle to survive in this nightmarish world of virals.<br /><br />One of my biggest issues with The Passage is the same issue I’ve had with this book; which is the fact that I really struggled to keep the characters in order. Granted all the characters had a wonderful amount of depth to them but because there are so many characters, it sometimes becomes a struggle keeping up with them. I had to write a list of each character and a bit of information about them just to refer back to and remember key aspects of their story arcs. While in The Twelve the characters seem to travel more as a big group so I didn’t feel like I needed a map and little figures to move around like a war map just to keep track of everyone.<br /><br />I love the post apocalyptic world the Justin Cronin has created and I found so much joy in returning to the world. He has created this wonderful world full of great characters, tension and action. But he has written these books in such literary way, which sets it apart from most Post-Apocalyptic survival novels. Although these books are bricks and too heavy to hold; they are still well worth reading.<br /><br />I’m a little worried that I now have to wait till 2014 for the final book in the trilogy, The City of Mirrors. Fans of The Passage, you are in for a treat with The Twelve; just for the joy of going back to the world and continuing the story but also because there are new characters to meet. While I think this book will stand out as a standalone read, I highly recommend you start this book from the beginning. But maybe wait till 2014 so you don’t have to suffer with waiting two years for the next instalment. I hope I haven’t given anything away about the plot away, but if you have read this book already I would love to hear your thoughts in the comments below.<br /><br />This review was originally found on my blog; <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://literary-exploration.com/2012/10/06/book-review-the-twelve/">http://literary-exploration.com/2012/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="289441460923226808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_462968734" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/462968734" />
    <a title="Ferdy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy"><img alt="Ferdy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487898103p2/4596358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/462968734?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ferdy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ferdy" href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy">Ferdy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780345534897" href="/book/show/13535471-the-twelve">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer462968734" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3220284666775794240"><b>Spoilers</b><br/><br/>-This was a chore to read, I struggled to finish it. The beginning was nonsensical and boring, the middle dragged and the ending was a mess. The back and forth in time didn't flow well, there was no rhyme or reason to the way events unfolded, one chapter was set in the present, the next 15 years in the past and then the next a 100 years in the past. It was all very disjointed and messy. The multiple POV's irritated me, most of the new character POV's bar Danny and Kitteridge were rubbish</span>
  <span id="freeText3220284666775794240" style="display:none"><b>Spoilers</b><br /><br />-This was a chore to read, I struggled to finish it. The beginning was nonsensical and boring, the middle dragged and the ending was a mess. The back and forth in time didn't flow well, there was no rhyme or reason to the way events unfolded, one chapter was set in the present, the next 15 years in the past and then the next a 100 years in the past. It was all very disjointed and messy. The multiple POV's irritated me, most of the new character POV's bar Danny and Kitteridge were rubbish, and quite a few of them were pointless. <br />-I wasn't impressed with the writing, it came across as arrogant and 'show offy', there was no need for the overly long and ridiculous descriptions of certain people, places and events. Also, the religious and preachy undertone to everything pissed me off.<br />-I couldn't always tell difference between the Twelve, Zero, the virals, the newer virals and the other virals. They weren't explained clearly and I wasn't sure how each of them came in to existence and which ones were more powerful.<br />-Amy and Peter's sudden love for each other was weird. In the last book Peter was in love with Alicia and she with him yet there wasn't really any reference to that in this book, they just acted like friends - When did they fall out of love? And how the hell did Peter fall in love with Amy? The whole Peter/Amy being in love was ridiculous. Amy was an interesting character in the The Passage but she was dull in this — all she cared about was the men in her life - Wolgast, Peter, the Twelve..ugh.<br />-I hated Lila, she was selfish, insufferable and horrid. The bond between her and Grey was hard to believe. Yes, they were in an extraordinary situation but Grey's devotion to Lila was bizarre since she was such a crazy bitch. I think the author was trying to portray Lila as mentally ill but I didn't believe it - her POV convinced me that she just felt sorry for herself and instead of facing up to things she chose to take the easy route and pretend everything was okay.<br />-Guilder was an awful character, he had no depth whatsoever - making characters have mummy, daddy and whore issues doesn't magically give a character a personality.<br />-I loathed the way the main female characters were portrayed: <br />-Amy, the girl with the super speshul powers and the one that was meant to save everyone was a nun/babysitter for most of the book - Why wasn't she a soldier with all her powers? Why wasn't she in the government or in the army making decisions? Why was that pretty much left to the male characters? Instead Amy had to be cooking, cleaning and taking care of a bunch of brats. When she did finally do something — it was to be a martyr and sacrifice her life for her wuv interest..ugh. <br />Alicia, the strong, powerful soldier ended up being raped, tortured and caged. What that added to the story or her character, I don't know. It didn't make her any stronger or weaker, it didn't make her change her priorities, it didn't make her any more hellbent on revenge, it didn't do anything. It was like the author just wanted to humiliate and demean her for a while because she was too confident and badarse and heaven forbid a fierce, strong and confident female character — only male characters can be powerful and sure of themselves.<br />Sara was raped, imprisoned in a camp and had her daughter stolen from her. Whereas her boyfriend was fine and dandy on the outside chilling out and working in a brothel. Sara had to earn her HEA through physical and emotional pain and torture whilst her husband in comparison was living in luxury.<br />And lastly Lila was beaten and controlled by a guy for decades, and on top of that she was selfish and insane.<br />So yea, 3 out of 4 female characters were brutalized by guys and the other female character died to save a guy — because women only change/are shaped/do things because of men/for men.<br />-I don't know if I'll bother reading the last book, this one was disappointing.��</span>

<div id="review_431309094" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/431309094" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/431309094?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=sci-fi-fantasy">sci-fi-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2012">read2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer431309094" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13403963623961979909">I wasn't going to read this book. I wasn't! I felt like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6690798.The_Passage__The_Passage__1_' title='The Passage (The Passage #1) by Justin Cronin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Passage</a> was a well-contained story and I didn't understand where else it could go. I will let the author explain what he focuses on in The Twelve, because I find it too difficult to summarize. (This is from an older post from 2010 on <a href='http://io9.com/5605835/justin-cronin-explains-his-vampires-in-the-passage-and-drops-spoilers-for-the-next-book' rel='nofollow noopener'>io9.com</a>.)<br/><br/><blockquote>The next two books each go back to Year Zero at the outset, to reset the story, and to deal with something you didn't see and didn't know was as important as it was. It's not a linear quest story, </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText13403963623961979909" style="display:none">I wasn't going to read this book. I wasn't! I felt like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6690798.The_Passage__The_Passage__1_" title="The Passage (The Passage #1) by Justin Cronin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Passage</a> was a well-contained story and I didn't understand where else it could go. I will let the author explain what he focuses on in The Twelve, because I find it too difficult to summarize. (This is from an older post from 2010 on <a href="http://io9.com/5605835/justin-cronin-explains-his-vampires-in-the-passage-and-drops-spoilers-for-the-next-book" rel="nofollow noopener">io9.com</a>.)<br /><br /><blockquote>The next two books each go back to Year Zero at the outset, to reset the story, and to deal with something you didn't see and didn't know was as important as it was. It's not a linear quest story, which I would find dull and plodding. With each book, you need to have the narrative terms reestablished with fresh elements. Also, if you didn't see [a character] die, they're not necessarily dead. There's a big cast in the first book, and plenty of unresolved stuff. I will resolve it by the end. [Early vampire character] Anthony Carter? No, not abandoning him.<br /><br />In [The Twelve], you go back to what happened in Denver after the outbreak took place. The story will resume in that location a few days after breakout. So you can see another angle on what occurred and certain elements will affect our band of heroes 100 years in the future. It will be called The Twelve - and it's not who you think.</blockquote><br />This means that the story starts with where Amy is, and follows up with an assortment of other characters. Just like in The Passage, storylines are dropped completely as others are followed. Since I was listening to the audio, it was a bit more difficult to keep track of, just because it was harder to flip back and get a refresher on names, etc. <br /><br />The author provides a lot more information about what happened to various people at the very beginning, explaining how some of the communities were formed, the horrific actions of the USA government (including events like "The Field"), and other parts of the novel jump around up to 97 years from when the virus originally took hold. This kind of information is usually my favorite part of post-apocalyptic stories - the rebuilding. What kind of societies form? How do they work? Who has control? I think Justin Cronin shows a lot of creativity and variety in these situations, since it isn't just one story, but multiple. Many of the characters, locations, and situations overlap throughout the story, and I had this sense of the author as a puppeteer, drawing strings of stories around each other. Kudos to him that they never seem to tangle in disaster.<br /><br />Scott Brick is the narrator for the audiobook of The Twelve, and does a fantastic job. He doesn't bother doing a lot of voices, but his inflection is perfect. He has this ability to get out of the way of the story that I really appreciate when I'm listening. It just comes to life and I'm not constantly thinking of HIM, but of the story.<br /><br />And The Twelve requires a lot of thinking and paying attention. The multiple story lines, the jumping around in time and history, and the sprinkling of quotations that Cronin throws in kept my attention. He started with a Mark Strand poem, almost as if I needed something to clinch whether or not I'd read this book.<br /><br />I won't have that dilemma for the final book. While this story has a satisfying climax, I was left with far more questions this time around. I'm not sure I know which side everyone is on. I'm not sure I even know what sides there <i>are</i>, anymore. That's about all I can say about it without spoiling!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13403963623961979909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_204268857" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/204268857" />
    <a title="Caroline " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3236194-caroline"><img alt="Caroline " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576541068p2/3236194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/204268857?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline " href="/user/show/3236194-caroline">Caroline </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=page-turner">page-turner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=he-wrote-it">he-wrote-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=memorable-characters">memorable-characters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer204268857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer226109152279582875">***ALL SPOILERS HIDDEN***<br/><br/>(Part I reviews <i>The Twelve</i>. Part II reviews the trilogy as a whole.)<br/><br/><u>Part I</u><br/>Sometimes the second book in a trilogy is merely a filler book that’s just a bridge between books one and three, nothing more. Not so with <i>The Twelve</i>. Fans of <i>The Passage</i> can rest assured that this second installment is a vital addition, smoothly continuing the epic Cronin started with book one. Beloved characters from <i>The Passage</i> are back, along with some captivating new ones, and, of course, the </span>
  <span id="freeText226109152279582875" style="display:none">***ALL SPOILERS HIDDEN***<br /><br />(Part I reviews <i>The Twelve</i>. Part II reviews the trilogy as a whole.)<br /><br /><u>Part I</u><br />Sometimes the second book in a trilogy is merely a filler book that’s just a bridge between books one and three, nothing more. Not so with <i>The Twelve</i>. Fans of <i>The Passage</i> can rest assured that this second installment is a vital addition, smoothly continuing the epic Cronin started with book one. Beloved characters from <i>The Passage</i> are back, along with some captivating new ones, and, of course, the virals are here in all their horrific glory. Cronin answered questions left unanswered in <i>The Passage</i>, and, notably, developed even further his take on the traditional vampire legend. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Really special about this second book in particular is his imagining of “degrees” of vampirism. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />The story is especially engrossing during well-imagined action sequences involving the virals and those scenes dominated by a three-dimensional villain readers will love to hate. It’s least engrossing during its military scenes. Here the story gets mired in militaristic details, jargon, logistics. This is a slog but is at least confined to the halfway point. Less forgivable <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[is the dragged-out ending, particularly the final battle scene. Cronin doesn’t seem able to write battle scenes clearly. The result is that this scene is disjointed and difficult to picture, eventually veering into the nonsensical when Amy transforms into a viral at will. Also problematic are plot holes such as these: How are 70,000 flatlanders able to sit in a stadium watching virals without getting harmed? Why don’t Alicia and the others shave their heads before posing as flatlanders? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br /><i>The Twelve</i>’s organization is different from <i>The Passage</i>’s in a way that can make it slightly harder to follow at times. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Whereas <i>The Passage</i> is divided into three tidy sections that are chronological, <i>The Twelve</i> is divided into numerous sections, switching back and forth among main characters. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <i>The Twelve</i>’s cast of characters is wide and various, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[and eventually most cross paths, their stories intertwining naturally and sometimes in fun and surprising ways. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Cronin upped the ante in this second book; <i>The Twelve</i> is perhaps more disturbing and violent than <i>The Passage</i>. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Nobody just dies. Characters are either ripped to pieces by virals--something readers already expect--or are killed via bombs, guns, strangulation, or some other violent means. Many blow themselves up. A few characters set themselves on fire. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Parts of <i>The Twelve</i> are gory, with some really sickening descriptions involving blood. Fortunately these aren’t overly long. <br /><br />Like <i>The Passage</i>, this is literary--intelligently and complexly written and thoughtful, very thoughtful. <i>The Twelve</i> is actually <i>too</i> musing sometimes; Cronin goes into the head of every single main character and remains there for one too many paragraphs. It’s clear he was aiming for a thriller/post-apocalyptic/horror story that can’t be summed up well--not just because it’s hard to categorize but because it’s so psychologically complex. <br /><br /><u>Part II</u><br /><i>The Passage</i> trilogy isn’t about vampires exactly, at least not in the way many people think when they think of vampire stories. These vampires most definitely aren’t of the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41865.Twilight?ac=1&amp;from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">Edward Cullen</a> variety or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/301082.Dead_Until_Dark" rel="nofollow noopener"> Bill and Eric</a> variety. They aren’t of the Dracula variety either. Cronin took a little here and a little there from different horror sources. Those who don’t like vampire stories, think again. This isn’t “another vampire story.” Cronin developed his human characters as much as his monsters, maybe more. <br /><br />Cronin plotted this trilogy from book one. The story arc therefore extends perfectly across all three books, concluding naturally in book three. Clues dropped and storylines begun in books one and two continue and are fleshed out fully in book three. Readers need to go in order; this is a <i>must</i>, as the books will be incomprehensible otherwise. These aren’t capsules. It’s also ideal--imperative--to read them back-to-back or at least very close in time. These contain many moving parts, and to wait too long between them is to forget numerous crucial details; furthermore, reading the books close in time packs the biggest punch. For those who did read them back-to-back, it can even be rewarding to go back to book one and reread at least part one of that. <br /><br />Cronin’s trilogy has proven to be on a much more elevated plane than others in the genre. There’s enough here to attract all kinds of readers, including those who normally eschew science fiction, horror, fantasy, or post-apocalyptic stories. There’s also enough artistry in Cronin’s writing to attract lovers of literary fiction. It’s truly a trilogy that can’t be summed up well, and that wouldn’t do it justice anyway. <br /> <br />Final verdict: A worthy second book in the trilogy that fans of <i>The Passage</i> can get excited about. <br /><br />(Final note: visit enterthepassage.com.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="226109152279582875" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11623508060858820038"><i>The Twelve</i> would have been a good read if only:<br/><br/>(1) the author would shut up about God and religion for a second.<br/><br/>(2) If said author hadn't been so insisting on telling us the life-story of unimportant minor characters and then killing them off later, then fully expecting us to give a damn about those minor characters.<br/><br/>(3) If said author isn't so insisted on humiliating his female characters by getting them raped, beat, captured, tortured and abused.<br/><br/>(4) Would everyone...just stop using rape as a pl</span>
  <span id="freeText11623508060858820038" style="display:none"><i>The Twelve</i> would have been a good read if only:<br /><br />(1) the author would shut up about God and religion for a second.<br /><br />(2) If said author hadn't been so insisting on telling us the life-story of unimportant minor characters and then killing them off later, then fully expecting us to give a damn about those minor characters.<br /><br />(3) If said author isn't so insisted on humiliating his female characters by getting them raped, beat, captured, tortured and abused.<br /><br />(4) Would everyone...just stop using rape as a plot device to make female characters hellbent on revenge and be badass!? There has to be other reasons and motivation for women to become badass!!<br /><br />(5) Even when women actually have power, still women cannot (i) become leaders, (ii) take control of their lives, (iii) care about things outside of their men, families and babies.<br /><br />Give me a break.<br /><br />Look at Amy, a young girl with supernatural power and the mystic connection with the vampires, has she joined the army and fight? Nap, instead she spent the last five years babysitting orphans as a nun.<br /><br />Look at Alicia, a capable, independent fighter with awesome fighting and survival skills, does she become a leader in the army? Nap, the other soldiers feel uncomfortable around her and she has no desire to be a leader. Yes, God forbid that a woman actually has any desire to lead! <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Last but not least, she got raped too, for no good reason. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Look at Sara, in this second book she spent years being a captive and was subjected to rape and slavery, whilst as her boyfriend has been spared the worst of the suffering. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Sara's determination for revenge is nice, but then the next thing we know, she found her supposedly dead daughter and her life is only revolved around the little girl. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Look at Lila, another woman with mystic connection with the vampires and she can control an army of vampires to do her bidding at will. But not only she is crazy, she also has no control over her own life, and when a man beat her up, she can only beg and cry.<br /><br />Comparing with what the women had suffered in the book, I think all the male characters are living quite a comfortable life.<br /><br />Oh, come on!<br /><br />(6) Last but not least, we have a couple of families taking their children outside of the protective walls against vampires so said children can enjoy <i>a picnic</i> in the great outdoor for the first time before vampires rushed into the field and ended their miserable lives.<br /><br />Too Stupid To Live.<br /><br />(7) Aside from the rape and how women in the story are treated, I'm pissed off by <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the BS about Amy and Peter falling in love!!!!! ARGHHH!!! Alicia/Peter is doing just fine in the first book, but Cronin just has to ruin it because, seriously how can a woman maintain a fulfilling romantic relationship when she has a mission, a career and proposes in life outside of her man and babies!? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I don't hate this book, but I don't like how women are treated in it, although the plots are exciting enough and the writing is pretty neat, but honestly...the novel is a bit unnecessarily long and if I weren't reading it in Chinese, I doubt I can finish the whole book. <br /><br />Review for book 1 here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6690798-the-passage">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6...</a> </span>

<div id="review_2238159692" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2238159692" />
    <a title="Stjepan Cobets" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44823204-stjepan-cobets"><img alt="Stjepan Cobets" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508476465p2/44823204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2238159692?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stjepan Cobets" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stjepan Cobets" href="/user/show/44823204-stjepan-cobets">Stjepan Cobets</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fan of horror, dystopia and Sci-Fi</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44823204-stjepan-cobets?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44823204-stjepan-cobets?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44823204-stjepan-cobets?shelf=apocalyptic-post-apocalyptic">apocalyptic-post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44823204-stjepan-cobets?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2238159692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13197662674826938327">My rating 4.3<br/><br/>The book “The Twelve (The Passage # 2)” by Justin Cronin is a solid follow-up to the first part. Stories of survivors blend in completely new ways. The world has not become better, perhaps it has become worse, and the fight against 11 major Viral continues. Honestly, this part I liked a bit more. This time the writer adds new characters, new events and to the core evil enemies. I would not talk about the main story but believe me, it will not leave you indifferent because the charac</span>
  <span id="freeText13197662674826938327" style="display:none">My rating 4.3<br /><br />The book “The Twelve (The Passage # 2)” by Justin Cronin is a solid follow-up to the first part. Stories of survivors blend in completely new ways. The world has not become better, perhaps it has become worse, and the fight against 11 major Viral continues. Honestly, this part I liked a bit more. This time the writer adds new characters, new events and to the core evil enemies. I would not talk about the main story but believe me, it will not leave you indifferent because the characters we follow from the first book have evolved and the story is spreading with new faces. Survival of the human species is the main essence of the book because Virals are one of the most dangerous enemies of the human, but they are also a human transformed into monsters. Who will die and who stay alive is questionable. I would recommend a book to horror lovers, the world of dystopia and Sci-Fi.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13197662674826938327" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1696979370" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1696979370" />
    <a title="Chad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25388909-chad"><img alt="Chad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589745633p2/25388909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1696979370?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chad" href="/user/show/25388909-chad">Chad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1696979370" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5780836685528965127">Cronin is a gifted writer who tells engrossing stories. Even when I'm confused, I still just want to read more. The book time shifts back and forth and it can exhausting keeping all the characters straight. Keep in mind that the past characters generally don't matter. Most of their stories don't continue on (even though I'd love to have known the rest of their stories). A large section of the book focuses on 4 different groups during the early days of the viral plague. Most of the book does cont</span>
  <span id="freeText5780836685528965127" style="display:none">Cronin is a gifted writer who tells engrossing stories. Even when I'm confused, I still just want to read more. The book time shifts back and forth and it can exhausting keeping all the characters straight. Keep in mind that the past characters generally don't matter. Most of their stories don't continue on (even though I'd love to have known the rest of their stories). A large section of the book focuses on 4 different groups during the early days of the viral plague. Most of the book does continue on with Amy and the rest of her friends from the Colony. They discover the Twelve has disappeared and eventually come across another city, this one some kind of concentration camp. The book ends in an entirely different direction than I expected and the series probably could have just ended here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5780836685528965127" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

(Woo!)<br/><br/>I enjoyed this one more than the first as, while it still had a lot of the same flaws (mainly the pacing issue), I found myself more engaged with the story and caring about the characters more in this volume.<br/><br/>Now, let's see if I can get through the final part of the trilogy while I'm on holiday...</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kqp0fMDB83RklSQf9Sv2mgokAvMZx5eRoOBuiVzJt3ICm8HD0f+Zb9A4LWUa0xastZtnPkF9/+BnPX4ZcBRTuw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ksy+NZkipouS/G3/BA5qff4r1r3/7MAx0cwk5D5MF3y6/QuKiBzMkCZRZIXr9opLQZSzcKdWqEAWETR0EpHztQ==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/45315.Justin_Cronin">About Justin Cronin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6887194844898277425">In 2010, Justin Cronin’s The Passage was a phenomenon. The unforgettable tale that critics and readers compared to the novels of Cormac McCarthy, Michael Crichton, Stephen King, and Margaret Atwood became a runaway bestseller and enchanted readers around the globe. It spent 3 months on The New York Times bestseller list. It was featured on more than a dozen “Best of the Year” lists, including Time</span>
  <span id="freeText6887194844898277425" style="display:none">In 2010, Justin Cronin’s The Passage was a phenomenon. The unforgettable tale that critics and readers compared to the novels of Cormac McCarthy, Michael Crichton, Stephen King, and Margaret Atwood became a runaway bestseller and enchanted readers around the globe. It spent 3 months on The New York Times bestseller list. It was featured on more than a dozen “Best of the Year” lists, including Time’s “Top 10 Fiction of 2010,” NPR’s “Year’s Most Transporting Books,” and Esquire’s “Best &amp; Brightest of 2010.” It was a #1 Indie Next Selection. It sold in over 40 countries and became a bestseller in many of them. Stephen King called The Passage “enthralling… read this book and the ordinary world disappears.” Now, PEN/Hemingway Award-winner Justin Cronin bring us the conclusion to his epic trilogy with The City of Mirrors. For the last time, Amy—the Girl from Nowhere, who lived a thousand years—will join her friends and face down the demons that threaten the last of humanity. Justin Cronin is also the author of Mary and O’Neil (which won the PEN/Hemingway Award and the Stephen Crane Prize), and The Summer Guest. Other honors for his writing include a fellowship from the National Endowment for the Arts and a Whiting Writer’s Award. A Distinguished Faculty Fellow at Rice University, he divides his time between Houston, Texas, and Cape Cod, Massachusetts. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6887194844898277425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
